  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_91bf56b9-4e97-48ef-a6ce-961300b5a46d'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2303651731" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2303651731" />
    <a title="Petrik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59458347-petrik"><img alt="Petrik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1568016775p2/59458347.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2303651731?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Petrik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Petrik" href="/user/show/59458347-petrik">Petrik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11394008-conqueror">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2303651731" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12790761632241270460"><b>I have mixed feelings regarding the final book of <i>Conqueror</i>, one of my favorite historical fiction series.</b><br/><br/>I’m starting to think that there’s a real curse contained within five books series that haunts me. I don’t even know why or how this happens, but I’m never left completely satisfied by the concluding installment of all five books series I’ve read so far. <i>Lightbringer</i> by Brent Weeks, <i>Heartstrikers</i> by Rachel Aaron, <i>The Shadow Campaigns</i> by Django Wexler, and unfortunately, this series. All of t</span>
  <span id="freeText12790761632241270460" style="display:none"><b>I have mixed feelings regarding the final book of <i>Conqueror</i>, one of my favorite historical fiction series.</b><br /><br />I’m starting to think that there’s a real curse contained within five books series that haunts me. I don’t even know why or how this happens, but I’m never left completely satisfied by the concluding installment of all five books series I’ve read so far. <i>Lightbringer</i> by Brent Weeks, <i>Heartstrikers</i> by Rachel Aaron, <i>The Shadow Campaigns</i> by Django Wexler, and unfortunately, this series. All of them, somehow, felt plagued with the same issue that they either felt too long or unnecessary to be one whole book. That’s the thing with <i>Conqueror: A Novel of Kublai Khan</i>, it felt more like a spin-off of the previous four books rather than a direct sequel or a concluding installment.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote> “What sort of a man would I be if I could just wipe out my errors with talking? A man has to live with his mistakes and go on. That is his punishment, perhaps.” </blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br /><i>Conqueror: A Novel of Kublai Khan</i> is the fifth and final book in the <i>Conqueror</i> series by Conn Iggulden. For the content and what this book is about, the title pretty much speaks for itself; this is a book about Kublai Khan and his rise to power. If you’ve been following my reviews for the past couple of weeks, you should already know that this is a series I genuinely love. Before I get down to the nitty-gritty of what makes this book disappointing to me, let me clarify once again that <i>Conqueror</i> is still one of my favorite historical fiction series of all time; what I feel regarding this final installment won’t change that. And please keep in mind that my assessment and feelings towards this book most likely fall on the unpopular opinion side; so many readers and fans of the series loved it.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote> “No worthy goal should come easily, he told himself. Suffering created value.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br />Let’s start with the main characters. As the title implied and prove, this is a book about Kublai Khan. Almost all of the previous main characters from the past four books are—understandably—no longer in the story here. This situation immediately meant that I’m reading about characters that I’m not invested in. In the previous four books, we followed the perspective of Genghis Khan, his brothers, his sons and Tsubodai, to name a few; these were characters we follow since their beginning and their first encounter. In the case of Genghis and his families, we read about them since they were a child and all of them received proper exposure and character development. Genghis Khan, for instance, has one book that centered on his coming-of-age story. Unfortunately, that isn’t the case with the main characters here; Kublai Khan, Hugelu, Mongke, and Arik-Bore were characters that haven’t received a lot of developments previously, and then suddenly to read from their perspectives IN the final installment of the series was jarring. Reading about Genghis Khan and the previous main characters was miles apart better than reading about the new main characters here.<br /><br />I have no idea whether it’s because I feel burned out by this series or not, that shouldn’t be the case because I still found myself completely hooked by <i>Khan: Empire of Silver</i>, but Iggulden’s writing here simply didn’t feel as engaging as his prose in the previous books. Plus, it felt like Iggulden tried to pack too much content into this one book and that disrupted the flow of the story. A lot of new characters introduced, important events like Hugelu’s brutal invasion of Baghdad didn’t receive enough exposure for lasting impact, and the battle scenes—which were A LOT and was superb before—somehow turned out to be a huge struggle to get through. All of these isn’t to say that Kublai Khan wasn’t interesting as a character, on the contrary, his distinctive personality from the other Khan and his insane achievements just needed more pages and proper development to shine through in the series that has focused the narrative on Genghis Khan, his brothers and his sons, and also Tsubodai for four books long.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote> “He was a man who loved his children and his people, Zhenjin. He took the Chin foot off the throat of the nation and made us look up from the struggles of tribes. He changed the world.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br /><i>Conqueror: A Novel of Kublai Khan</i> feels more like a spin-off that follows different characters and new storylines rather than a direct sequel, because of that, my mixed feelings regarding this book ended up becoming inconsequential towards my enjoyment and love of the previous books of the series. Seriously, this isn’t one of those “the last book of the series ruined my love for the entire series” case, <b><i>Conqueror</i> is still up there in my list of favorite historical fiction series together with <i>The Warlord Chronicles</i> by Bernard Cornwell</b>. I honestly recommend reading the previous four books of the series with all my heart, none of them ended on a cliffhanger and they’re easily some of the most engrossing (and informational) books—all genres combined—I’ve read this year. And although I’ve only voiced my criticism for this book in this review, I did have a good time in several sections of the book, especially the beginning and ending sequences. Without a doubt, I will be reading more of Iggulden’s work in the future, no idea when yet, but I’m leaning towards reading his <i>Wars of the Roses</i> series. <br /><br />Here’s one last passage from Conn Iggulden himself about the <i>Conqueror</i> series and why you SHOULD read it:<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“This story began with a single, starving family, hunted and alone on the plains of Mongolia – and ends with Kublai Khan ruling an empire larger than that of Alexander the Great or Julius Caesar. Over just three generations, that is simply the greatest rags-to-riches tale in human history.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <u>Series Review:</u>
</b><br /><br /><i>Genghis: Birth of an Empire</i>: <u>
  <b>
    <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2303651605?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">5/5 stars</a>
  </b>
</u><br /><i>Genghis: Lords of the Bow</i>: <u>
  <b>
    <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2303651635?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">4/5 stars</a>
  </b>
</u><br /><i>Genghis: Bones of the Hills</i>: <u>
  <b>
    <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2303651671?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">5/5 stars</a>
  </b>
</u><br /><i>Khan: Empire of Silver</i>: <u>
  <b>
    <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2303651703?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">4.5/5 stars</a>
  </b>
</u><br /><i>Conqueror: A Novel of Kublai Khan</i>: <b>
  <u>3/5 stars</u>
</b><br /><br /><i>Conqueror</i>: <b>
  <u>21.5/25 stars</u>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer14570240296777858931">Conn Iggulden’s latest book chronicles the life of Kublai Khan, son of Tolui, grandson of Genghis Khan. <br/><br/>A warrior who would rule a fifth of the world with strength and wisdom.<br/><br/>A scholar who conquered an empire larger than those of Alexander or Caesar.<br/><br/>A brother who betrayed his own to protect a nation. <br/><br/>Conqueror paints a vivid picture of Mongol life post-Genghis and Iggulden masterfully brings almost legendary characters to life. As with all of his novels, he inserts many details from everyday lif</span>
  <span id="freeText14570240296777858931" style="display:none">Conn Iggulden’s latest book chronicles the life of Kublai Khan, son of Tolui, grandson of Genghis Khan. <br /><br />A warrior who would rule a fifth of the world with strength and wisdom.<br /><br />A scholar who conquered an empire larger than those of Alexander or Caesar.<br /><br />A brother who betrayed his own to protect a nation. <br /><br />Conqueror paints a vivid picture of Mongol life post-Genghis and Iggulden masterfully brings almost legendary characters to life. As with all of his novels, he inserts many details from everyday life, which makes the story more authentic-feeling. He masterfully puts believable motivations behind all of his historical figures, which is the part of his writing that I love best.<br /><br />The way he writes makes reading one of his novels an almost cinematic experience and he makes history accessible to the ordinary person. Another success of Iggulden’s writing, which is especially apparent in this novel, is his avoidance of creating a story that focuses too heavily on events, dates and geography. In Conqueror, the subject of exploration is the mind of the Kublai Khan from formative years through to a great civil war against his own brothers. As such, this novel appeals to those interested in history from a statistic as well as humanistic perspective. More generally, Iggulden’s incredible depiction of landscapes and battles should draw in readership from fans of thrillers and fantasy alike</span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8340830856871426504">5 books, all 5*'s. By far the best historical fiction series I've read. <br/><br/>The thing CI gets is balance. He doesn't try to minimize the raw violence which was the Mongolian horde but he doesn't make them into mindless killers either.   <span id="freeText8340830856871426504" style="display:none">5 books, all 5*'s. By far the best historical fiction series I've read. <br /><br />The thing CI gets is balance. He doesn't try to minimize the raw violence which was the Mongolian horde but he doesn't make them into mindless killers either. They have hopes, dreams, families, fears, needs and endure physical and mental pain to accomplish their goals. <br /><br />Likewise CI balances giving us a sense of their culture and the cultures they conquer without getting carried away and boring everybody this side of a sociologist or anthropologist to tears. The moving tribe and their supply lines, the women and children, the need for food in places where it's scarce at best. <br /><br />We also get a really good sense of what it was like to have the horde sitting outside your gates knowing you were damned no matter what choices you made. <br /><br />An epic tale that made you feel for people on both sides. <br /><br />I'm currently also reading his Emperor series which is good but not close to this series. I'll definitely checkout War of the Roses after that. </span>

<div id="review_3152824925" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3152824925" />
    <a title="Zitong Ren" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/77983193-zitong-ren"><img alt="Zitong Ren" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552188473p2/77983193.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3152824925?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zitong Ren" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zitong Ren" href="/user/show/77983193-zitong-ren">Zitong Ren</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007271160" href="/book/show/11739337-conqueror">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77983193-zitong-ren?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77983193-zitong-ren?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77983193-zitong-ren?shelf=action">action</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77983193-zitong-ren?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77983193-zitong-ren?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77983193-zitong-ren?shelf=not-fantasy-2020">not-fantasy-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3152824925" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8945814986529549846">I thought, like many other readers that this was a great conclusion to the series, which surprisingly is, from the top of my head, the first historical fiction series that I have finished that has a large number of books in it. Many of the historical fiction have actually been standalones, or having maybe two or three books in the series, despite me really enjoying historical fiction as a genre. So if anyone knows any good historical fiction series, please recommend them to me.<br/><br/>Like the rest of t</span>
  <span id="freeText8945814986529549846" style="display:none">I thought, like many other readers that this was a great conclusion to the series, which surprisingly is, from the top of my head, the first historical fiction series that I have finished that has a large number of books in it. Many of the historical fiction have actually been standalones, or having maybe two or three books in the series, despite me really enjoying historical fiction as a genre. So if anyone knows any good historical fiction series, please recommend them to me.<br /><br />Like the rest of the series, it didn’t feel quite like a five star read to me(I mainly give stars based off personal feeling towards a book more than anything), but was certainly a solid four. For some reason, I felt like that maybe the books won’t be as memorable as I would like, which may be a factor of the constant warring in every book, whereas I would have liked a bit more character development, though again, I know the author is trying his best to recreate historical figures here, and of that, I think he has done an amazing job. <br /><br />There are certainly many books, often usually YA that, due to the audience, or maybe just style of the author, that some things can be left out, such as the brutal treatment of woman or that slaughter the Mongols inflicted among the people. It is stunningly brutal and I normally really dislike reading scenes like this, but I’m glad all the same that the author put these things in so that we can really experience the horror and almost relive in a way what these people have gone through. Just reading stuff like the Sacking of Baghdad really hurts in an odd way, seeing the ancient city, with the library getting destroyed, not to mention the hundreds of thousands that were slaughtered because of it. I thank him for putting such stuff in, despite the fact that it does almost make me wince at the brutality of the Mongols and their harsh treatment of people.<br /><br />Another thing I liked about historical fiction is that it actually gets me googling up stuff on the topics and things in these books. I already am an avid learner of history and learning more stuff on these things is a whole load of fun. It also gets me the chance to look at maps, lots of maps, which, being both a fantasy and map nerd, is also, lots of fun to do. Even just reading more about each region’s history and such are good things as it gives me wider knowledge of the world.<br /><br />I certainly plan to read more of Iggulden’s books in the future such as finishing off his series of Julius Caesar, which I think should be great. 8/10</span>
  <a data-text-id="8945814986529549846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review3152824925">

<div id="review_885694940" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/885694940" />
    <a title="David Sven" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8802423-david-sven"><img alt="David Sven" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1402220807p2/8802423.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/885694940?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sven" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sven" href="/user/show/8802423-david-sven">David Sven</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11394008-conqueror">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8802423-david-sven?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer885694940" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10103935693467546475">A good finale to the series. This book sees the rise of Kublai Khan. Growing up, nobody would have guessed it would be the scholar grandson of Genghis, with his head in books and adoption of Chin culture, who would turn out to be Gur Khan of the Mongols - Then again, a generation or two before that nobody foresaw that there would be such a thing as a Mongol scholar.<br/><br/><br/>Of course, Kublai doesn't stay a scholar, forced to take the field by his brother Mongke to extend Mongol control beyond the Chin bo</span>
  <span id="freeText10103935693467546475" style="display:none">A good finale to the series. This book sees the rise of Kublai Khan. Growing up, nobody would have guessed it would be the scholar grandson of Genghis, with his head in books and adoption of Chin culture, who would turn out to be Gur Khan of the Mongols - Then again, a generation or two before that nobody foresaw that there would be such a thing as a Mongol scholar.<br /><br /><br />Of course, Kublai doesn't stay a scholar, forced to take the field by his brother Mongke to extend Mongol control beyond the Chin borders into Sung territory. Kublai's very non Mongol custom of sparing the cities he conquered saw a lot of of the Sung forts open their gates wide without seeing any battle. Having some very competent generals as well as learning and honing his own skill in war strategy and tactics served Kublai later on well when he wrested control of the Empire from his brothers.<br /><br />Again, Conn Iggulden weaves a fascinating tale of war and intrigue with a very event oriented plot. At the end of the book Iggulden includes some historical notes to let us know where he indulged in artistic licence. He pretty much sticks close to the facts. <br /><br />Having the main characters as actual historical figures does mean the story feels a lot like it's on rails at times with the plot dictated by actual historical events. If I contrast this to Bernard Cornwell's style of writing, I find Cornwell gives himself a lot more room to maneuver by having his main characters as grunts in an army, and then weaving the story around one or two major historical events. In this way he is able to invent and develop his own characters while still referencing actual events in history and I think I prefer this signature Cornwell style.<br /><br />Still, I have enjoyed this series immensely and highly recommend it to any historical fiction buffs who like a good battle/war oriented story or are interested in Mongol history in general.<br /><br /><br />4 stars<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10103935693467546475" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa Reads &amp; Reviews" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa Reads &amp; Reviews" href="/user/show/5395843-lisa-reads-reviews">Lisa Reads &amp; Reviews</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385343053" href="/book/show/11526977-conqueror">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5395843-lisa-reads-reviews?shelf=first-reads">first-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5395843-lisa-reads-reviews?shelf=historical">historical</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer241471656" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6659854681466036106">I never considered myself a reader of historical fiction novels, probably due to the manner in which history was/is taught in school. In college, however, I had a favorite professor, educated at Harvard, with a keen intellect, and wit. During a class, and I still remember this, he mentioned the Mongols' sacking of Baghdad, where the city folks were so materialistic, they swallowed their jewels, only to have their bellies split open and their treasures robbed. Tremendous libraries and precious ar</span>
  <span id="freeText6659854681466036106" style="display:none">I never considered myself a reader of historical fiction novels, probably due to the manner in which history was/is taught in school. In college, however, I had a favorite professor, educated at Harvard, with a keen intellect, and wit. During a class, and I still remember this, he mentioned the Mongols' sacking of Baghdad, where the city folks were so materialistic, they swallowed their jewels, only to have their bellies split open and their treasures robbed. Tremendous libraries and precious art were gleefully destroyed. Civilians were slaughtered and the city's treasury was plundered. The event was an extreme example of cultured civilization falling under swords of ruthlessness.<br /><br />Well. I suppose that lecture was the reason I impulsively entered the Goodreads giveaway for Conn Iggulden's novel about Kublai Khan. It is a long, rich, finely researched, description of bloody battles, Mongolian strategy, barbarian character, and strength that lifted a family from a roving band of starving nomads to, by the third generation, conquerors of areas greater than those of Alexander the Great and Julius Caesar. If you would have gauged my level of interest in such things only a week ago, I would have yawned. Now, I'm going to read the first of the Iggulden's series about Genghis Khan, and perhaps the rest of the series as well. Goes to show, you never know a person, until they read a good book, and their interests are set ablaze. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6659854681466036106" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3594123449" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3594123449" />
    <a title="Debbie Zapata" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22110057-debbie-zapata"><img alt="Debbie Zapata" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451631863p2/22110057.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3594123449?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Debbie Zapata" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Debbie Zapata" href="/user/show/22110057-debbie-zapata">Debbie Zapata</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385343053" href="/book/show/11526977-conqueror">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22110057-debbie-zapata?shelf=2020printbooks">2020printbooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3594123449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1347078248122462774">Oct 16, 1pm ~~ Review asap.<br/><br/>Oct 17, 2pm ~~ I thought I might come into this review spouting all sorts of intelligent remarks about not only this books but the entire series. I have opinions about it all, of course, but I am no scholar or philosopher so I will spare everyone those thoughts about nation building, power, corruption, greed; how one man's vision shifts when he is no longer there to guide it, and the influences of different cultures on a new nation. If the accepted leader of your count</span>
  <span id="freeText1347078248122462774" style="display:none">Oct 16, 1pm ~~ Review asap.<br /><br />Oct 17, 2pm ~~ I thought I might come into this review spouting all sorts of intelligent remarks about not only this books but the entire series. I have opinions about it all, of course, but I am no scholar or philosopher so I will spare everyone those thoughts about nation building, power, corruption, greed; how one man's vision shifts when he is no longer there to guide it, and the influences of different cultures on a new nation. If the accepted leader of your country prefers to live as if he had been born in a different culture, how true can he really be to yours?<br /><br />Okay, well, I said I was not going to get into all of that. But you see some of the ideas that these books triggered. <br /><br />I always like books about how a person becomes the person they are meant to be. In this book we see Kublai Khan transform himself from scholar to warrior to Khan. Heavily influenced by Chin culture, but still Khan of all Mongolia. Times change over the generations, of course. The weapons of war had 'improved' since the days of Genghis, but horsemen, their bows and their incredible endurance were still feared and necessary tools of power. <br /><br />I am glad I read these books. In school we learned a little about Genghis, then there was just a confusing bunch of names until Kublai showed up. Here we get to see what happened in between the two. Perhaps the books were not always completely accurate (the author admits this himself in his notes a the end of each book, pointing out places he altered timelines and so on) but I think we get an idea here of what it takes to create a nation and then keep it together. <br /><br />The creating belonged to Genghis. The keeping together fell to his sons and grandsons. We leave the nation with Kublai in charge, his battles behind him, ready to face the future. While I do think a book about his years would be interesting, I agree with the author that we should leave Kublai at the moment we do: taking the reins of the nation his grandfather brought into being in the first book of the series. It feels appropriate. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="1347078248122462774" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <a title="Terri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Terri" href="/user/show/1968826-terri">Terri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1968826-terri?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1968826-terri?shelf=asia-fiction">asia-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer423637430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10447252578103869970">I have gone off this series by Conn Iggulden. I appreciated Book 1 and 2, but I have not felt anything for the books since then, and this is book 5, so that tells you how long it has been since I have gotten into this series.<br/> I have persisted in the hopes that sooner or later I would feel what I felt in Book 2 and because I keep buying the damn books so that I have the collection. Nice covers make me weak.<br/><br/>I find the writing quality in many of Iggulden's books simplistic and inconsistent. Like a </span>
  <span id="freeText10447252578103869970" style="display:none">I have gone off this series by Conn Iggulden. I appreciated Book 1 and 2, but I have not felt anything for the books since then, and this is book 5, so that tells you how long it has been since I have gotten into this series.<br /> I have persisted in the hopes that sooner or later I would feel what I felt in Book 2 and because I keep buying the damn books so that I have the collection. Nice covers make me weak.<br /><br />I find the writing quality in many of Iggulden's books simplistic and inconsistent. Like a slow building headache. <br />It is not always wanting though, there are moments where it is perfectly fine, but then it would stumble and fumble along, and I would find myself wincing often.<br />One of the major issues I had was with the low brow blood and guts description. It was the equivalent of a B Grade slasher movie. Violence for the sake of violence. Description of blood splattering and people dying in every possible way.<br />This form of description is for a different class of reader than I. I don't go "haw haw..that's some bloody ripping good shit!" when someone's slashed up, gutted, exploding in parts, dismembered, disembowelled, crushed and broken.<br /><br />Moving on. <br /><br />The book was too long. It needed shaving down. Too much blow by blow of nothing of any great significance, too much soap opera and too much (as mentioned) description of violence. <br />Hiding within the pages of Conqueror was a decent book crying to be let out. Instead, it was smothered in cheese and a fair amount of corn.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_218812644" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/218812644" />
    <a title="Lance Greenfield" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2793431-lance-greenfield"><img alt="Lance Greenfield" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442658682p2/2793431.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/218812644?book_show_action=true">Oct 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lance Greenfield" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lance Greenfield" href="/user/show/2793431-lance-greenfield">Lance Greenfield</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780007285433" href="/book/show/12079884-conqueror">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer218812644" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13581996677280476071">This is a wonderful conclusion to the series. Once I was into the last one hundred pages, I just had to keep reading until I had finished at 2.30 am this morning.<br/><br/>As Conn Iggulden says in his historical notes at the end of the book, there is still so much that he could have continued to write about the Mongolian empire. Sorhatani, mother of the four grandsons of Genghis: Mongke, Kublai, Hulegu and Arik-Boke, deserves a book of her own. However, I think that Conn is correct in deciding to leave th</span>
  <span id="freeText13581996677280476071" style="display:none">This is a wonderful conclusion to the series. Once I was into the last one hundred pages, I just had to keep reading until I had finished at 2.30 am this morning.<br /><br />As Conn Iggulden says in his historical notes at the end of the book, there is still so much that he could have continued to write about the Mongolian empire. Sorhatani, mother of the four grandsons of Genghis: Mongke, Kublai, Hulegu and Arik-Boke, deserves a book of her own. However, I think that Conn is correct in deciding to leave the story at the point that he does. <br /><br />In this book, three of the four sons of Sorhatani, and Tolui, become Gur-khan (the Great Khan), following the early demise of their cousin Guyuk. As the author points out, this is a work of fiction, and the exact nature of any of the deaths and battles are made up around known historical fact. This is waht makes Conn Iggulden such a great writer of historical fiction. He brings it to life. He adds excitement to the (possibly) dry historical reference. This is why I love the genre so much.<br /><br />Although it is possible to read any one of the five books in this series as a stand-alone volume, I would advise against it. One can only wonder at the rise of an empire that, at its peak, extends far further than either the Roman or Alexandran empires, from the tiniest of beginnings in the first episode. Terujin, as a small boy, is purssued into the wilderness. He grows to become Genghis, uniting the Mongolian nation and conquering most of the known world from western Europe to Eastern China.<br /><br />The Conqueror series is a marathon read, and I have read them as they hit the book shops. I am glad that I did. This is tremendous series, and Conn Iggulden and his supporters have done a fantastic job.<br /><br />I thoroughly recommend the whole series starting from the first volume and ending in the early hours of one morning sometime in your future. You will not be disappointed!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13581996677280476071" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1611840235" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1611840235" />
    <a title="Sud666" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36890366-sud666"><img alt="Sud666" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1418437112p2/36890366.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1611840235?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sud666" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sud666" href="/user/show/36890366-sud666">Sud666</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36890366-sud666?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1611840235" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17703402104882713282">Conn Iggulden follows up his excellent series on Genghis khan with another wonderful tale. This time it's about Genghis's grandson- Kublai. It follows the intelligent and different, from the Mongol norm, man from his young teenage years to his brother Mongke becoming Khan and sending Kublai on a conquest of the Southern Sung lands of China. The book does also track the experiences of Mongke, Hulegu, Batu and others. <br/><br/>As per his norm, Mr. Iggulden fills his pages with well researched and exciting </span>
  <span id="freeText17703402104882713282" style="display:none">Conn Iggulden follows up his excellent series on Genghis khan with another wonderful tale. This time it's about Genghis's grandson- Kublai. It follows the intelligent and different, from the Mongol norm, man from his young teenage years to his brother Mongke becoming Khan and sending Kublai on a conquest of the Southern Sung lands of China. The book does also track the experiences of Mongke, Hulegu, Batu and others. <br /><br />As per his norm, Mr. Iggulden fills his pages with well researched and exciting tales. While, there are a few changes for the plot (the head of the Nizari Islamili Order at Alamut was NOT named Sulieman but rather Al Aud-Din), on the whole the story is historically accurate. It is also exciting and flows very well. The sheer range of the action from Russia to Baghdad to South China is phenomenal. He also understands the differing personalities of the grandsons of Ghenghis and how that affects their actions. Coupled with a good knowledge of how the Mongol's fought battles, from strategy to tactical formations- this a book that will not only entertain, but inform anyone interested in the Mongols.<br /><br />I can not recommend this highly enough. Mr. Iggulden's work is amongst the best historical fiction I have read in a long while.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17703402104882713282" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2278412383" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7366522678130546590">'This story began with a single, starving family, hunted and alone on the plains of Mongolia - and ends with Kublai Khan ruling an empire larger than that of Alexander the Great or Julius Caesar. Over just three generations, that is the simply the greatest rags-to-riches tale in human history.'-Conn Iggulden<br/><br/>Conqueror is second only to the first book in this series as my favorite. It's just a well written novel and I could tell Conn put his heart into this one. In addition to what is usually guar</span>
  <span id="freeText7366522678130546590" style="display:none">'This story began with a single, starving family, hunted and alone on the plains of Mongolia - and ends with Kublai Khan ruling an empire larger than that of Alexander the Great or Julius Caesar. Over just three generations, that is the simply the greatest rags-to-riches tale in human history.'-Conn Iggulden<br /><br />Conqueror is second only to the first book in this series as my favorite. It's just a well written novel and I could tell Conn put his heart into this one. In addition to what is usually guaranteed from this author (great battles, epic moments, heart moving scenes), I really enjoyed the political intrigue here. The play for Power, and how it can change you (usually for the worse) once achieved. <br /><br />"Power is always dangerous, it attracts the worst and corrupts the best"-Ragnar, Vikings.<br /><br />The princes Mongke, Kublai, Hulegu and Ariq-Boke are in the spotlight. We see their differences in the way they deal with situations. But we clearly see what binds them as brothers: An Iron will. <br />I particularly enjoyed Kublai and Hulegu's sections.<br /><br />In a time filled with clashing swords and ambitions, treachery, vengeful assassins...a Conqueror will rise.<br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1721945329" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1721945329" />
    <a title="KB" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1318729-kb"><img alt="KB" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1338161514p2/1318729.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1721945329?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="KB" class="user" itemprop="url" name="KB" href="/user/show/1318729-kb">KB</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007271153" href="/book/show/15768118-conqueror">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1721945329" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3084269726081732339">In the fifth and final book of the Conqueror series, there is a lot going on. Lots of names. Lots of death. Lots of places. Like the second and fourth books, I can't say I loved it in its entirety, but it was still a good read and a satisfying end to the series.<br/><br/>All the books had plenty of characters to keep track of, but I think this one maybe overdid it a bit. I think this is partly because you're exposed to so much over a fairly short span. This wasn't like the beginning of the series where we</span>
  <span id="freeText3084269726081732339" style="display:none">In the fifth and final book of the Conqueror series, there is a lot going on. Lots of names. Lots of death. Lots of places. Like the second and fourth books, I can't say I loved it in its entirety, but it was still a good read and a satisfying end to the series.<br /><br />All the books had plenty of characters to keep track of, but I think this one maybe overdid it a bit. I think this is partly because you're exposed to so much over a fairly short span. This wasn't like the beginning of the series where we slowly got to know the characters over the course of a few books. Many of those in <i>Conqueror</i> really only become substantial characters in this last part.<br /><br />As I said, lots of people die in this book and there constantly seems to be an issue over who will become khan next. This culminates in the civil war between Khubilai and his younger brother Ariq Boke. Khubilai's campaign against the Song in China is also a focal point of the book.<br /><br /><i>Conqueror</i>, in many ways, provided what I'd come to expect from Iggulden: great characterization, interesting dialogue, good scene setting and description of places. He really puts you in the moment for the most part. I can't say this book, or any in the series, gave me a hard time trying to envision anything.<br /><br />I did like that Iggulden stopped the series with Khubilai becoming khan, which is where we end part five. I was getting a little burnt out partway through this book and I don't know if I would've wanted to read another two or three to finish the story. I think it segues nicely into the actual history, too. Want to know what happens next? Just go pick up a book. <s>Or go watch Marco Polo, I guess.</s><br /><br />One thing I found a little disappointing, as this is the end of the series, was that many characters don't get a real ending. I believe Iggulden did make a comment in the previous book about how some of these names just fade from history, but when you're writing fiction you can make educated guesses to provide some closure. I'm positive he just stopped writing in Jebe at some point. After an excellent conversation between Subedei and Batu early in the book, we learn Subedei had retired to live as a herdsman and then we don't see him again. He was in four of the five books, and I was hoping there would have been some comment on his death. And Batu as well. I found him quite irritating in the previous book, but in Conqueror I thought Iggulden was taking him in an interesting direction but then we don't see much of him after a certain point either.<br /><br />While I didn't agree with all of Iggulden's choices in fictionalizing the history, some additions were quite interesting and overall I liked how he characterized and brought to life all of these historical figures. <br /><br />And even though, in my opinion, there was some fluctuation in the quality of the books, I'd still recommend the series. I'm not sure if I'd pick up anything by Iggulden again - I don't think I could do another series if it's not something I'm heavily interested in - but I enjoyed him as a writer and appreciated his knowledge of the history. These books were a lot of fun to read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3084269726081732339" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText7290164717050623579" style="display:none">I received this book from the Goodreads Firstreads program. I was very excited to receive the book as I love Historical Fiction and always wanted to find out more about the Mongols. When requesting the book, I did not realize that it was part of a series. However, I am now happy that I have a new author to catch up on. I plan on going back and reading the entire Conqueror series, and also the Emperor series. <br /><br />I really enjoyed how the author presented the historical information and how he developed the characters. I had a vivid picture of the physical setting of the story in my mind as I read. The author used a writing style that was descriptive yet still moved the story forward at a good pace. I had some concerns that I would be a little lost since I had not read the previous books in the series. However, a little bit of research brought me up to speed on the historical setting and I settled in for a great read.<br /><br />Oh, I also found out that Conn Igguleden is the co-author of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/204097.The_Dangerous_Book_for_Boys" title="The Dangerous Book for Boys by Conn Iggulden" rel="nofollow noopener">The Dangerous Book for Boys</a>. A must read for young readers.</span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer230549016" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9505221144187521823">Conn Iggulden makes a splendid return to his 13th century world of Mongol dominance. "Conquerer" marks Iggulden's fifth book in his historical fiction series on Genghis Khan, his legacy, and the lasting cultural impact of his Mongol empire on the world. <br/> <br/>"Conquerer" focuses on one of Genghis' grandsons, Kublai Khan, who's probably most well known for his monumental interactions with explorer Marco Polo. Absent of the Marco Polo years, "Conquerer" explores the rise of Kublai Khan from a studious </span>
  <span id="freeText9505221144187521823" style="display:none">Conn Iggulden makes a splendid return to his 13th century world of Mongol dominance. "Conquerer" marks Iggulden's fifth book in his historical fiction series on Genghis Khan, his legacy, and the lasting cultural impact of his Mongol empire on the world. <br /> <br />"Conquerer" focuses on one of Genghis' grandsons, Kublai Khan, who's probably most well known for his monumental interactions with explorer Marco Polo. Absent of the Marco Polo years, "Conquerer" explores the rise of Kublai Khan from a studious and intellectual youth, to a savvy general and Khan of one of the largest empires our world has ever seen.<br /> <br />The first 120 pages set the stage for Kublai's assencion. Iggulden imagines the politics of the ugly internecine battles for leadership among Genghis' grandchildren (and their wives). The palace (and ger) intrigues expose Iggulden's deft writings skills outside the context of adventurous battle scenes. This may come as a disappointment to readers used to his magnificently large scale wars which come to the more patient reader further into the book. <br /> <br />It's been a year since I read "Khan: Empire of Silver", the fourth book in the series which focuses on the reign of Genghis' son Ogedai, and Iggulden only provides the most subtle clues as to the back story of characters and family alliances. I had to shake the cobwebs to recapture where things had left off in the previous book and this may make things a little frustrating to the new reader. In the broader context of this sweeping tale, it makes little difference to the understanding of the story of Kublai. <br /> <br />Two generations after Ghengis, the Mongols sought stability at their core while continuing the process of expansion started two generations earlier. It was also a time when the Mongol nation was modernizing, mostly through the influences of their pan-Asian conquests, especially China. <br /> <br />Kublai's brother Monkge assumes control of the Mongol Empire and returns to it the discipline and strength instilled initially by his grandfather Genghis. Mongke asks Kublai to put down his scrolls and pens and pick up a bow to finish his grandfather's work in China.<br /> <br />In the book's second act, Iggulden shifts focus between the Mongol frontiers in the far west and south as we follow the parallel stories of Kublai and his brother Hulegu. As Kublai captures a small walled city in China, Hulegu prepares to take Baghdad, the light of the Muslim world. Iggulden writes each event from two perspectives: Kublai and the city's Prefect; Hulegu and Baghdad's Caliph. Each battlefront throughout the novel follows a similarly satisfying pair of perspectives. <br /> <br />Hulegu is vicious and deceptive. Kublai has a keen intelligence while taking a very different approach to conquest. Hulegu is the very model of the Mongol reputation for unforgiving violence while Kublai takes a more measured approach by allowing those that surrender to live. He’s slowly building out his future constituency, while Hulegu’s ironfisted approach will ultimately lead to a strongly rebellious people and the ejection of the Mongols from the middle east.<br /> <br />Kublai transforms from an intellectual into a battle-hardened, glory-seeking leader. His foundation of cultural intellectualism continues to drive him. Hulegu, like his predecessors, is very "foreign", cold, distant and harshly regal. Kublai is infinitely more relatable. He’s loving father and husband, and his committed adoption to Chinese culture weighs heavily in his favor with the Chinese people.<br /> <br />Kublai’s tale is, in many ways, a tale of the evolution of the Mongol empire. He represents the transformation of a society from a massive blending of distinct tribes from across the steppe into a forward-thinking, multicultural world influencing power. <br /> <br />As always, Iggulden's writing is very big and bold. Dialogue is straight forward, but more subtle when Kublai is involved. Like the other books in this series, the reader is introduced to the world and culture of the Mongols and, to a lesser extent, 13th century China. If this is the last book in this series then I’m genuinely sad to see it conclude. This is a good book in a very strong series. <br /><br />I received this book as part of Amazon's Vine program.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9505221144187521823" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_229318090" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/229318090" />
    <a title="Susan Johnson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5172713-susan-johnson"><img alt="Susan Johnson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583444420p2/5172713.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/229318090?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan Johnson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan Johnson" href="/user/show/5172713-susan-johnson">Susan Johnson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer229318090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6199548503473114483">This novel fittingly ends the Khan series. The author says that he wants to end a series with the character alive and Kublai is very much alive and in control at the book's ending. This series has been so interesting and I have learned so much about the Mongols although, if truth be told, I knew next nothing when I began. Genghis was a fascinating man who was a natural military intellect. As much as I was interested in his life, I had no desire to emulate it. Riding the plains for months, eating</span>
  <span id="freeText6199548503473114483" style="display:none">This novel fittingly ends the Khan series. The author says that he wants to end a series with the character alive and Kublai is very much alive and in control at the book's ending. This series has been so interesting and I have learned so much about the Mongols although, if truth be told, I knew next nothing when I began. Genghis was a fascinating man who was a natural military intellect. As much as I was interested in his life, I had no desire to emulate it. Riding the plains for months, eating hard bread and cold meat, drinking the blood of my horse and engaging in battle holds no appeal for me but how great it is to learn about somebody so completely different from me. <br />The weskest book was "Silver", the one before this one. It was not the author's fault. The subject matter just wasn't as interesting. It's a very hard act to follow Genghis and, in fact, any great man. You rarely find a son from true leaders who are as heroic as their fathers. How can they be? they grew up differently and, usually, with a lot less hardship and they suffer for it. That's what happened in "Silver". <br />But that brings us to this book, the story of the grandsons and what a group they were. I think there could have many fine khans. Batu, Jochi's son, could have been great but the line came through Tolui and Sorahatani, a great ruler in her own right. Mongke becomes the first khan of that family and shows flashes of brillance. He sends one brother west to Baghdad, Kublai to China, one to guard the home front and his able cousin, Batu, to Russia. In one sweeping move he gives his natural opponent to the Khanate enough to satisfy their ambitions and keep them busy. Problem solved. <br />The middle third of the book bogs down a little as Kublai fights his way across the Sung empire. He uses quite a few tricks he has learned from studying Genghis' battles. I guess all that studying finally paid off. It was great to discover what happened to the Chin Emperor in exile. <br />After several unfortunate deaths, two brothers, Kublai and Arik-Boke, fight each other for the ultimate prize, Khan. Mongol against mongol! Unheard of and, yet, it happened. Other rulers were forced to make difficult choices on who to support. Although, history tells us the outcome, I won't spoil it for anyone. The book does end up on a rather "up" note which is a nice change. <br />This has been a rwarding book, and series, to read. I have appreciated learning so much about a period that I knew next to nothing about. I was pleasantly surprised to find a female Mongol leader I admired. Who would have thought? <br />The author, in his historical notes, sums it up best. He says, "The story begins with a single, starving family hunted and alone on the plains of Mongolia- and ends with Kublai Khan ruling an empire larger than that of Alexander the Great or Julius Caesar. Over just three generations, that is the greatest rags-to-riches tale in human history." I couldn't have said it better.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6199548503473114483" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3162214103" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3162214103" />
    <a title="Amber" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21965967-amber"><img alt="Amber" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1449092263p2/21965967.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3162214103?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amber" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amber" href="/user/show/21965967-amber">Amber</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21965967-amber?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21965967-amber?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21965967-amber?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21965967-amber?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21965967-amber?shelf=male-authors">male-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21965967-amber?shelf=testosterone-poisoning">testosterone-poisoning</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21965967-amber?shelf=dude-lit">dude-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21965967-amber?shelf=war-stories">war-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21965967-amber?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3162214103" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6579528578321731044">I read this as a stand-alone novel even though it's officially Book 5 of a series, and it seems to stand well on its own – in fact, judging from the complaints of some reviewers that it doesn't fit into the rest of the series that well, maybe it's actually better as a standalone.<br/><br/>It's filled with explicit descriptions of a lot of battles, so... graphic violence warning! Right away, you're hit over the head with the fact that this is going to be a bloody, violent story. The opening scene has Guyuk</span>
  <span id="freeText6579528578321731044" style="display:none">I read this as a stand-alone novel even though it's officially Book 5 of a series, and it seems to stand well on its own – in fact, judging from the complaints of some reviewers that it doesn't fit into the rest of the series that well, maybe it's actually better as a standalone.<br /><br />It's filled with explicit descriptions of a lot of battles, so... graphic violence warning! Right away, you're hit over the head with the fact that this is going to be a bloody, violent story. The opening scene has Guyuk, the Khan-presumptive, casually murdering an underling who makes him angry. Expect that tone to continue, but you will also find that there is a clear line drawn between the truly brutal men who see other human beings as little more than places to sheath their swords, and those who are more thoughtful. And outside the battles, the scenes like the opening scene, and a few other specific ones, are there not just for shock value, but to show you, “This dude = bad guy.”<br /><br />Our protagonist, Kublai, is not like that. Perhaps surprisingly for a Mongol warleader, Kublai is portrayed as intelligent (even bookish), thoughtful, down to earth, temperate, a kind and loving husband and father, and basically just a decent, likable guy. In fact, he shows such an unconventional interest in letting enemies live once they surrender, that there's considerable doubt for a while whether he has what it takes to be a success as a Mongol. But he does go on to prove that mercy does not mean weakness.<br /><br />It's interesting to follow the progress of Kublai through northern China and his brother Hulagu through Persia, carrying out the orders of their older brother, Mongka Khan. While they both use a tactic of “surrender to me quickly and live; resist me and die,” both the results and the feelings they inspire are quite different. Kublai conquers a land whose culture he respects and which he wants keep intact, rule himself, and see prosper, while Hulagu cares nothing for the culture or the people he is conquering and wants only to wring as much wealth from them as possible before he puts them to the torch. Hulagu's progress through Persia, and in particular his sack of Baghdad, became so infamous modern-day Muslim authors are still talking about its effect on the Muslim world. See <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6240926.Destiny_Disrupted_A_History_of_the_World_Through_Islamic_Eyes" title="Destiny Disrupted A History of the World Through Islamic Eyes by Tamim Ansary" rel="nofollow noopener">Destiny Disrupted: A History of the World Through Islamic Eyes</a>. <br /><br /><b>Does it pass the Bechdel test?</b> No. There are a couple of significant women in the early part of the story, but because all their political power derives from their sons, the sons are their only topic of conversation. And they both exit the stage before the 25% mark. The utter lack of women makes me want to go read a story about what life was like for the <i>other</i> half of the Mongol people.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6579528578321731044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2740302465?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2740302465" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14490706477610965685">Super conclusion to an epic series. So much happens in this book that it felt like it could have been made into two separate books :-) This guy writes an addictive story and brings such fascinating historical characters to life. Now i definitely know about the great Mongol empire. <br/><br/>This book is like a dramatic change into the progress of Mongol empire as it gives the insight into the thoughts behind the actions of Kublai Kahn, Gengis Kahn's brightest grandson. Kublai chooses to establish and flou</span>
  <span id="freeText14490706477610965685" style="display:none">Super conclusion to an epic series. So much happens in this book that it felt like it could have been made into two separate books :-) This guy writes an addictive story and brings such fascinating historical characters to life. Now i definitely know about the great Mongol empire. <br /><br />This book is like a dramatic change into the progress of Mongol empire as it gives the insight into the thoughts behind the actions of Kublai Kahn, Gengis Kahn's brightest grandson. Kublai chooses to establish and flourish in previously unconquered Chinese territory. He chooses to offer peaceful surrender rather than military devastation by his armies; the devastation which the then-world remembers all too vividly from the time of his grandfather's violent victories. Kublai spent his learning years being guided and educated about the advantages of the Chin culture by a Chin monk who had been Gengis' adviser. It is Kublai's goal to achieve domination of this culture to divert their riches into his coffers and unending streams of riches and knowledge for himself, his heir and his followers. This change in the though process goes a long way in changing the direction of the kingdom and the world. <br /><br />Really enjoyed this series. Long, but engaging and I also enjoyed the notes at the end, where the author clarifies some of the "license" he took with the story. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14490706477610965685" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2740302465">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_2903945204" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2903945204" />
    <a title="Bruno" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/97396628-bruno"><img alt="Bruno" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570964679p2/97396628.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2903945204?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bruno" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bruno" href="/user/show/97396628-bruno">Bruno</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/97396628-bruno?shelf=conqueror-series">conqueror-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/97396628-bruno?shelf=top-10-favourite-works-in-2019">top-10-favourite-works-in-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/97396628-bruno?shelf=british-literature">british-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/97396628-bruno?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/97396628-bruno?shelf=favourite-foreign-works">favourite-foreign-works</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2903945204" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14463296062393234815">The final book in the series excellent, both as the continuation of the Genghis khan's line, as well as a standalone title. Until reading it, I didn't have a clue what kind of a person Kublai khan was. The only reference was that he and the great Marco Polo met each other and that they entered into a trade negotiations. Fortunately, this book focuses on his military career, as well as his vision of reforming the empire. But, the third generation of Mongols is not without it's flaws, as there are</span>
  <span id="freeText14463296062393234815" style="display:none">The final book in the series excellent, both as the continuation of the Genghis khan's line, as well as a standalone title. Until reading it, I didn't have a clue what kind of a person Kublai khan was. The only reference was that he and the great Marco Polo met each other and that they entered into a trade negotiations. Fortunately, this book focuses on his military career, as well as his vision of reforming the empire. But, the third generation of Mongols is not without it's flaws, as there are many pretenders to the throne in the likes of his brothers and potential cousins. What is so fascinating is that this book doesn't revolve just around him, as there are fights on the other side of the world as well as inner conflicts. Also, I mustn't forget the military commanders who accompany them, as they are way more experienced on the field of battle and are dedicated to their ruler, even under the unexpected situations. I'm glad that the series has ended on a high note, but I'm also sad because it's over and the author has no intention to continue it. If you're in a search of a story that will keep you occupied for a month, I highly recommend this series. It's a great lesson of moral virtues that we need to oblige and what we should try to avoid, so we may prosper.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14463296062393234815" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
